Propositions: Categorical and Conditional
Author defines proposition and divides it into categorical and conditional, then subdivides conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between categorical and conditional propositions and identify conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Every proposition is either a simple predication or a compound with a condition — the key to logical analysis.

In logic, before we evaluate arguments, we must understand the building blocks: propositions (qadiyya). This passage defines the proposition and divides it into two fundamental types: categorical (ḥamliyya) and conditional (sharṭiyya). Within conditionals, we further distinguish conjunctive (muttaṣila) from disjunctive (munfaṣila). Mastering these divisions is essential for analyzing the structure of arguments in Islamic jurisprudence, theology, and grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of proposition
Notice that a proposition is defined as a statement (qawl) that can be described as true or false. This is the genus-differentia definition: genus = statement, differentia = truth-aptness.
In logic, only truth-bearing statements count as propositions.
2
Apply the resolution criterion
To determine if a proposition is conditional, check whether removing a connector (like 'if' or 'either') yields two complete propositions. If yes, it is conditional; otherwise, it is categorical.
The connector is often called the 'link' (rābiṭ).
3
Distinguish conjunctive vs disjunctive conditionals
A conjunctive conditional (muttaṣila) asserts that the truth or falsity of one proposition depends on another, e.g., 'If it is human, then it is animal.' A disjunctive conditional (munfaṣila) asserts incompatibility (tanāfi) between two propositions in truth and falsity together, e.g., 'This number is either even or odd.'
The disjunctive example shows strict exclusion: both cannot be true nor both false.
4
Examine the examples given
The passage provides explicit examples: 'If it is human, then it is animal' (conjunctive), and 'This number is either even or odd' (disjunctive). Also note the negation of a disjunctive: 'It is not that this is either an animal or black.'
The negative disjunctive is called ḥaqīqiyya sāliba.
5
Note the incomplete subdivision
The passage begins to mention further subtypes (likely māni‘at al-jam‘ and māni‘at al-khuluw) but cuts off. Understand that these are more specific disjunctives based on whether they exclude conjunction, exclusion, or both.
The lesson is based only on the given text; continue to next pages for complete classification.

Source Text

فصل في تعريف القضايا وأقسامها وما يتعلق بها القضية قول يقال لقائله إنه صادق أو كاذب، وهي شرطية إن انحلت إلى قضيتين بعد حذف الرابط، وإلا فحملية. فالشرطية إما متصلة وهي التي يحكم فيها بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على تقدير أخرى، كقولنا إن كان هذا إنسانا فهو حيوان وليس إن كان هذا إنسانا فهو جماد، وإما منفصلة وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين القضيتين في الصدق والكذب معا، وتسمى حقيقية موجبة كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج أو فرد، أو بنفيه وتسمى حقيقية سالبة كقولنا ليس هذا إما أن يكون حيوانا أو أسود، أو في
Section on the definition of propositions, their divisions, and what pertains to them. The proposition is a statement of which the speaker is said to be truthful or false. It is conditional if it can be resolved into two propositions after removing the connector, otherwise it is categorical. The conditional is either conjunctive (muttaṣil), which is that in which one judges the truth or falsity of one proposition upon the condition of another, like our saying 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' and 'It is not that if this is a human, then it is inanimate'; or disjunctive (munfaṣil), which is that in which one judges the incompatibility between the two propositions in truth and falsehood together, and it is called true affirmative (ḥaqīqiyya mūjaba) like our saying 'This number is either even or odd', or the negation of it called true negative (ḥaqīqiyya sāliba) like our saying 'It is not that this is either an animal or black', or in...

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) as a statement (qawl) that can be true or false. It then divides propositions into two main types: conditional (sharṭiyya) and categorical (ḥamliyya). A conditional proposition can be resolved into two component propositions after removing the connecting particle (e.g., 'if', 'either'), while a categorical cannot. The author then subdivides conditionals into conjunctive (muttaṣil) and disjunctive (munfaṣil). A conjunctive conditional asserts that the truth or falsity of one proposition depends on another, exemplified by 'If it is human, then it is an animal'. A disjunctive conditional asserts a relation of incompatibility (tanāfi) between two propositions in truth and falsity. Two types of disjunctive are given: the affirmative true disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya mūjaba) like 'This number is either even or odd', which is true because they cannot both be true or false together (they fully exclude each other), and the negative true disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya sāliba) which negates such a disjunction, like 'It is not that this is either an animal or black'. The passage cuts off as it begins to mention further subtypes (likely مانعة الجمع and مانعة الخلو).

Key Terms
قضية (qadiyya): A statement (qawl) that can be described as true or false.
شرطية (sharṭiyya): A proposition that can be resolved into two propositions after removing the connector (e.g., 'if', 'either').
حملیة (ḥamliyya): A categorical proposition that predicates something of something without a condition.
متصلة (muttaṣila): Conjunctive conditional: one proposition is true or false given another.
منفصلة (munfaṣila): Disjunctive conditional: there is incompatibility between two propositions in truth and falsity.
حقيقية موجبة (ḥaqīqiyya mūjaba): An affirmative true disjunction where the two propositions cannot be both true or both false.
حقيقية سالبة (ḥaqīqiyya sāliba): A negative true disjunction that denies a true disjunction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a disjunctive conditional?
A) If it is human, then it is animal.
B) This number is either even or odd.
C) Every human is mortal.
D) It is not that if it is human, then it is inanimate.

Key Takeaway
The proposition is defined by truth-aptness, and its division into categorical and conditional (with conjunctive and disjunctive subtypes) is foundational for all subsequent logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is a categorical or conditional proposition: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' (categorical); 'If the sun rises, then it is day.' (conditional conjunctive); 'Either this animal is a bird or a mammal.' (conditional disjunctive).
?
Take a verse from the Qur'an, e.g., 'If you are in doubt about what We have sent down to Our servant, then produce a surah like it' (Q. 2:23). Determine whether this is a conjunctive or disjunctive conditional. (Conjunctive, because the command to produce depends on the condition of doubt.)

Classification of Propositions and Conditionals
Author defines proposition and its division into conditional and categorical, then subtypes conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a proposition as conditional or categorical and identify the specific type of conditional (conjunctive or disjunctive with subtypes).

Core Idea
Propositions are truth-bearing units; conditionals decompose into two propositions with distinct truth relations.

This passage introduces the fundamental building blocks of logical reasoning: propositions (qadiyya). The author defines a proposition as a statement that can be true or false, then divides it into conditional and categorical types. Conditional propositions are further distinguished into conjunctive (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or), with disjunctives having subtypes based on truth conditions. Mastering this classification is essential for analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of a proposition
A proposition is a statement that can be judged true or false. Not all sentences are propositions—only declarative ones. In Islamic logic, this excludes questions and commands.
The Arabic term qadiyya can have broader meanings in other contexts; here it is strictly declarative.
2
Distinguish conditional from categorical
Test a proposition by removing the connector (rabit). If it resolves into two complete propositions, it is conditional; otherwise, it is categorical.
Example: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' resolves into 'this is a human' and 'it is an animal'.
3
Identify conjunctive vs. disjunctive conditionals
Conjunctive conditionals (mutasila) use 'if...then' and assert truth on condition. Disjunctive conditionals (munfasila) use 'either...or' and assert mutual exclusion.
Conjunctive: 'If the sun rises, it is day.' Disjunctive: 'The number is either even or odd.'
4
Classify disjunctive subtypes
Disjunctives have four subtypes: haqiqiyya mūjiba (exclusive or), haqiqiyya saliba (negation of exclusive), mani'at al-jam' (inclusive or, prevents both true), and mani'at al-khuluw (prevents both false so at least one true).
Check if the disjunction rules out both true, both false, or both together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية قول يقال لقائله إنه صادق أو كاذب، وهي شرطية إن انحلت إلى قضيتين بعد حذف الرابط، وإلا فحملية. فالشرطية إما متصلة وهي التي يحكم فيها بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على تقدير أخرى، كقولنا إن كان هذا إنسانا فهو حيوان وليس إن كان هذا إنسانا فهو جماد، وإما منفصلة وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين القضيتين في الصدق والكذب معا، وتسمى حقيقية موجبة كقولنا هذا العدد إما زوج أو فرد، أو بنفيه وتسمى حقيقية سالبة، كقولنا ليس هذا إما أن يكون حيوانا أو أسود، أو في الصدق فقط، وتسمى مانعة الجمع كقولنا هذا إما إنسان أو فرس، أو في الكذب فقط، وتسمى مانعة الخلو كقولنا زيد إما أن يكون في البحر أو لا يغرق.
The proposition is a statement of which it is said to its utterer that it is true or false. It is conditional if it resolves into two propositions after removing the connector, otherwise it is categorical. The conditional is either conjunctive, in which one judges the truth or untruth of a proposition on condition of another, like our saying 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' and 'It is not that if this is a human, then it is inanimate'; or it is disjunctive, in which one judges the mutual exclusion of the two propositions in truth and falsehood together, called affirmative exclusive, like our saying 'This number is either even or odd', or by its negation called negative exclusive, like our saying 'It is not that this is either an animal or black', or in truth only, called 'preventer of conjunction' like our saying 'This is either a human or a horse', or in falsehood only, called 'preventer of vacuum' like our saying 'Zayd is either in the sea or not drowning'.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines a proposition (qadiyya) as a statement that admits truth or falsehood. It then divides propositions into conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya). A conditional resolves into two propositions after removing the connector. Conditionals are further divided into conjunctive (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). For conjunctive, an example is 'If this is a human, then it is an animal.' The disjunctive has several subtypes: 'affirmative exclusive' (haqiqiyya mūjiba) which excludes both truth and falsehood together (e.g., even/odd), 'negative exclusive' (saliba) which negates such mutual exclusion, 'preventer of conjunction' (mani'at al-jam') which excludes only truth together (e.g., being human or horse), and 'preventer of vacuum' (mani'at al-khuluw) which excludes only falsehood together (e.g., being in the sea or not drowning). The examples are drawn from everyday logic, showing the range of conditional relations.

Key Takeaway
Proposition classification—conditional vs. categorical and their subtypes—provides the foundation for understanding logical inference in the Islamic sciences.

Three Components of a Categorical Proposition
Author breaks down categorical propositions into their three parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and copula in any categorical proposition in Arabic.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has three parts: subject, predicate, and the copula linking them.

In Islamic logic, arguments are built from propositions. To analyse a proposition, you must first recognise its essential building blocks: the thing you are talking about (subject), what you say about it (predicate), and the relation that connects them (copula). This lesson will help you see these parts clearly, whether the copula is expressed or implied.

Guided Path
1
Locate the subject (mawḍū‘)
The subject is what the proposition is about. Ask: 'What is being judged?' In 'Zayd is knowledgeable', the subject is Zayd. In Arabic sentences without an explicit copula, the subject is still the first element (e.g., 'Zayd knowledgeable').
The subject may be a specific individual or a universal term.
2
Find the predicate (maḥmūl)
The predicate is what is affirmed or denied of the subject. In 'Man is an animal', the predicate is 'animal'. It is the second main element.
The predicate is always a term (sometimes a phrase) that is judged of the subject.
3
Identify the copula (rābiṭa)
The copula expresses the judgmental relation. In tripartite propositions it is a separate word like 'is' (huwa) or 'is not' (laysa). In bipartite propositions it is omitted, but the relation is still understood. Check whether the proposition is tripartite (explicit copula) or bipartite (implicit copula).
Even when omitted, the proposition still has a relation; it is just not expressed by a separate word.
4
Determine affirmation or negation
If you can truthfully say 'the subject is the predicate', the proposition is affirmative (like 'Man is an animal'). If you can truthfully say 'the subject is not the predicate', it is negative (like 'Man is not a stone'). The presence of 'not' (laysa or lā) indicates negation.
Negation can be in the copula (e.g., 'Zayd is not knowledgeable') or in the quantifier (e.g., 'No fire is cold').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحملية يتحقق بأجزاء ثلاثة موضوع أعني: محكوما عليه ومحمول - أعني: محكوما به ونسبة بينهما وتسمى نسبة حكمية، واللفظ الدال عليها يسمى رابطة كهو في زيد هو عالم وتسمى حينئذ ثلاثية، وقد تحذف الرابطة في بعض اللغات وتسمى حينئذ ثنائية كزيد عالم . وهي موجبة إن صح أن يقال: إن الموضوع محمول، كقولنا الإنسان حيوان وسالبة إن صح أن يقال: إن الموضوع ليس بمحمول ، كقولنا الإنسان ليس بحجر. فموضوعها إن كان شخصا معينا سميت مخصوصة وشخصية، وإن كان كليا فإن بين فيها مقدار أفراد الموضوع سميت محصورة ومسورة، واللفظ الدال عليه يسمى سورا، وهي إما موجبة كلية وسورها (كل) كقولنا كل نار حارة، أو سالبة كلية وسورها (لا شيء) و (لا) (واحد) كقولنا : لا شيء ولا واحد من الناس بجماد، أو موجبة جزئية، وسورها (بعض) و (واحد) كقولنا بعض الحيوان أو واحد منه إنسان، وسالبة جزئية، وسورها (ليس كل) و(ليس بعض) و(بعض ليس). وإن لم يبين فإن لم تصلح القضية لأن تقصد بها كلية وجزئية سميت طبيعية، وإلا فمهملة كقولنا الحيوان جنس والإنسان لفي خسر، والمهملة في قوة الجزئية.
The categorical proposition consists of three parts: a subject, meaning that about which something is judged; a predicate, meaning that which is judged; and a relation between them called the judgmental relation, and the word indicating it is called the copula, like 'is' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. It is then called tripartite. The copula may be omitted in some languages, and it is then called bipartite, like 'Zayd knowledgeable'. It is affirmative if it is correct to say that the subject is the predicate, like our saying 'Man is an animal', and negative if it is correct to say that the subject is not the predicate, like our saying 'Man is not a stone'. Its subject, if it is a specific individual, is called particular and personal; if it is universal, and if the extent of the individuals of the subject is indicated in it, it is called quantified and quantified, and the word indicating it is called quantifier. It is either universal affirmative, whose quantifier is 'every', like our saying 'Every fire is hot'; or universal negative, whose quantifier is 'none' and 'no' and 'not one', like our saying 'No one and none of the people is inanimate'; or particular affirmative, whose quantifier is 'some' and 'one', like our saying 'Some animal or one of them is human'; and particular negative, whose quantifier is 'not every', 'not some', and 'some are not'. If it is not specified, then if the proposition is not suited to be intended as universal or particular, it is called natural, otherwise it is called unquantified, like our saying 'Animal is a genus' and 'Man is indeed in loss', and the unquantified has the force of the particular.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage details the structure of categorical propositions. A categorical proposition has three essential parts: subject (mawḍū‘), predicate (maḥmūl), and the relation between them, which is expressed by a copula (rābiṭa). When the copula is present, e.g., 'Zayd is knowledgeable', the proposition is called tripartite (thulāthiyya). When the copula is omitted, e.g., 'Zayd knowledgeable', it is bipartite (thunā’iyya). The proposition is affirmative if the predicate is affirmed of the subject, negative if denied. The subject can be a specific individual (making it personal, shakhṣiyya) or universal. For universal subjects, if the quantity (extent) of the individuals is indicated, the proposition is quantified (maḥṣūra) and the quantifier (sūr) is used. Four quantifier types: universal affirmative (kull), universal negative (lā shay’, lā, wāḥid), particular affirmative (ba‘ḍ, wāḥid), particular negative (laysa kull, laysa ba‘ḍ, ba‘ḍ laysa). If quantity is not indicated, the proposition is either 'natural' (ṭabī‘iyya) if it cannot be intended as universal or particular (e.g., 'Animal is a genus'), or 'unquantified' (muhmala) otherwise (e.g., 'Man is indeed in loss'), and the unquantified is treated as having the force of a particular proposition.

Key Terms
موضوع
محمول
نسبة حكمية
رابطة

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition always has a subject, predicate, and a judgmental relation; the copula may be explicit or implicit.

Quantifiers and Types of Quantified Propositions
Author explains quantifiers and the four quantified forms, plus natural and unquantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify categorical propositions as universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative based on their quantifier.

Core Idea
Quantifiers (sūr) tell us how many of the subject the predicate applies to: all, none, some, or not all.

Not all propositions are created equal: some talk about all individuals of a subject, some about none, some about some, and some are left unspecified. The quantifier (sūr) is the key that unlocks the proposition's logical power. In this lesson, we will learn the four standard quantified forms and the special cases of natural and unquantified propositions.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the proposition is quantified
Look for a quantifier word like 'kull' (every), 'lā shay'' (none), 'ba‘ḍ' (some), or 'laysa kull' (not every). If present, the proposition is called maḥṣūra (quantified). If no quantifier is present, check if it is natural (ṭabī‘iyya) or unquantified (muhmala).
Personal propositions (e.g., 'Zayd is standing') are not quantified; they are treated as singular.
2
Identify the quantifier word
List the quantifier words: for universal affirmative: 'kull' (كل); universal negative: 'lā shay'' (لا شيء), 'lā' (لا), 'wāḥid' (واحد); particular affirmative: 'ba‘ḍ' (بعض), 'wāḥid' (واحد); particular negative: 'laysa kull' (ليس كل), 'laysa ba‘ḍ' (ليس بعض), 'ba‘ḍ laysa' (بعض ليس). Match the word you see.
Some quantifiers like 'wāḥid' can be ambiguous; context determines if it means 'one' (particular) or 'no one' (universal negative).
3
Classify into one of four types
Based on the quantifier, classify the proposition as: (1) Universal Affirmative (kull), (2) Universal Negative (lā shay' etc.), (3) Particular Affirmative (ba‘ḍ, wāḥid), (4) Particular Negative (laysa kull, ba‘ḍ laysa). Example: 'Every fire is hot' is universal affirmative; 'No man is inanimate' is universal negative.
The classification directly matches the quantifier and the presence of negation.
4
Handle unquantified and natural propositions
If no quantifier appears, check if the proposition is 'natural' (ṭabī‘iyya): these are statements that are not intended to be quantified, like 'Animal is a genus'. If it could be intended as universal or particular, it is 'unquantified' (muhmala) and is treated as having the force of a particular proposition (e.g., 'Man is in loss' is taken as 'Some men are in loss').
The natural proposition is not quantified; the unquantified is treated as particular for logical purposes.

The categorical proposition consists of three parts: a subject, meaning that about which something is judged; a predicate, meaning that which is judged; and a relation between them called the judgmental relation, and the word indicating it is called the copula, like 'is' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. It is then called tripartite. The copula may be omitted in some languages, and it is then called bipartite, like 'Zayd knowledgeable'. It is affirmative if it is correct to say that the subject is the predicate, like our saying 'Man is an animal', and negative if it is correct to say that the subject is not the predicate, like our saying 'Man is not a stone'. Its subject, if it is a specific individual, is called particular and personal; if it is universal, and if the extent of the individuals of the subject is indicated in it, it is called quantified and quantified, and the word indicating it is called quantifier. It is either universal affirmative, whose quantifier is 'every', like our saying 'Every fire is hot'; or universal negative, whose quantifier is 'none' and 'no' and 'not one', like our saying 'No one and none of the people is inanimate'; or particular affirmative, whose quantifier is 'some' and 'one', like our saying 'Some animal or one of them is human'; and particular negative, whose quantifier is 'not every', 'not some', and 'some are not'. Commentary
This passage details the structure of categorical propositions. A categorical proposition has three essential parts: subject (mawḍū‘), predicate (maḥmūl), and the relation between them, which is expressed by a copula (rābiṭa). When the copula is present, e.g., 'Zayd is knowledgeable', the proposition is called tripartite (thulāthiyya). When the copula is omitted, e.g., 'Zayd knowledgeable', it is bipartite (thunā’iyya). The proposition is affirmative if the predicate is affirmed of the subject, negative if denied. The subject can be a specific individual (making it personal, shakhṣiyya) or universal. For universal subjects, if the quantity (extent) of the individuals is indicated, the proposition is quantified (maḥṣūra) and the quantifier (sūr) is used. Four quantifier types: universal affirmative (kull), universal negative (lā shay’, lā, wāḥid), particular affirmative (ba‘ḍ, wāḥid), particular negative (laysa kull, laysa ba‘ḍ, ba‘ḍ laysa). If quantity is not indicated, the proposition is either 'natural' (ṭabī‘iyya) if it cannot be intended as universal or particular (e.g., 'Animal is a genus'), or 'unquantified' (muhmala) otherwise (e.g., 'Man is indeed in loss'), and the unquantified is treated as having the force of a particular proposition.

Key Terms

محصورة
مهملة
طبيعية

B) بعض

Quantified propositions fall into four standard types; unquantified propositions are treated as particular unless they are natural.

The Three Parts of a Categorical Proposition
Definition of the categorical proposition and its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and relation (copula) in any categorical proposition and distinguish between tripartite and bipartite forms.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has a subject, predicate, and a hidden or explicit relation (copula).

In this passage, the author breaks down the categorical proposition into its essential parts. Understanding these parts is the first step to analysing any logical statement. We learn that the copula (like 'is') may be explicit or omitted, and we must be able to identify it even when it is hidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Find the entity that is being talked about (mawdu'). In 'Zayd is knowledgeable', 'Zayd' is the subject. Ask: 'What is this proposition about?'
The subject is also called mahkum 'alayh (that which is judged upon).
2
Identify the predicate
Find what is being said about the subject (mahmul). In 'Zayd is knowledgeable', 'knowledgeable' is the predicate. It is mahkum bihi (that which is judged with).
3
Find the relation (copula)
Look for the word that expresses the connection between subject and predicate. In 'Zayd is knowledgeable', 'is' is the copula. In Arabic, it may be omitted (e.g., 'Zayd knowledgeable' = Zaydun 'alimun). Determine if the proposition is tripartite (explicit copula) or bipartite (implicit copula).
The relation is also called nisba hukmiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحملية يتحقق بأجزاء ثلاثة موضوع أعني: محكوما عليه ومحمول أعني: محكوما به ونسبة بينهما وتسمى نسبة حكمية، واللفظ الدال عليها يسمى رابطة كهو في زيد هو عالم وتسمى حينئذ ثلاثية، وقد تحذف الرابطة في بعض اللغات وتسمى حينئذ ثنائية كزيد عالم. وهي موجبة إن صح أن يقال: إن الموضوع محمول، كقولنا الإنسان حيوان وسالبة إن صح أن يقال: إن الموضوع ليس بمحمول، كقولنا الإنسان ليس بحجر. فموضوعها إن كان شخصا معينا سميت مخصوصة وشخصية، وإن كان كليا فإن بين فيها مقدار أفراد الموضوع سميت محصورة ومسورة، واللفظ الدال عليه يسمى سورا، وهي إما موجبة كلية وسورها (كل) كقولنا كل نار حارة، أو سالبة كلية وسورها (لا شيء) و (لا) (واحد) كقولنا : لا شيء ولا واحد من الناس بجماد، أو موجبة جزئية، وسورها (بعض) و (واحد) كقولنا بعض الحيوان أو واحد منه إنسان، وسالبة جزئية، وسورها (ليس كل) و(ليس بعض) و(بعض ليس). وإن لم يبين فإن لم تصلح القضية لأن تقصد بها كلية وجزئية سميت طبيعية، وإلا فمهملة كقولنا الحيوان جنس والإنسان لفي خسر، والمهملة في قوة الجزئية؛ إذ متى صدق الإنسان لفي خسر، صدق بعض الإنسان لفي خسر، وبالعكس.
The categorical proposition is realized with three parts: a subject, meaning that which is judged upon; a predicate, meaning that which is judged with; and a relation between them, called a judgmental relation. The word indicating it is called a copula, like 'is' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable', and then it is called tripartite. The copula may be omitted in some languages and then called bipartite, like 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. It is affirmative if it is correct to say that the subject is the predicate, like 'Man is an animal'; and negative if it is correct to say that the subject is not the predicate, like 'Man is not a stone'. If its subject is a specific individual, it is called particular or individual; if it is universal, then if the scope of the individuals of the subject is specified, it is called quantified and determined, and the word indicating it is called a quantifier. It is either universal affirmative with its quantifier 'every' like 'Every fire is hot'; or universal negative with its quantifier 'nothing' and 'no' and 'none' like 'Nothing and no one of people is inanimate'; or particular affirmative with its quantifier 'some' and 'one' like 'Some animal or one of them is human'; and particular negative with its quantifier 'not every' and 'not some' and 'some not'. And if it is not specified, then if the proposition is not suitable to be intended as universal or particular, it is called natural; otherwise it is indeterminate, like 'The animal is genus' and 'Man is indeed at loss'. The indeterminate is in force of the particular, because whenever 'Man is at loss' is true, 'Some man is at loss' is true, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya) and its classification. The author begins by stating that a categorical proposition consists of three essential parts: the subject (mawdu‘), the predicate (mahmul), and the relation (nisba) between them, which is called a judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya). The word that expresses this relation is the copula (rabita), e.g., 'is' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. When the copula is explicit, the proposition is called tripartite; when omitted (as in Arabic often), it is called bipartite, e.g., 'Zayd is knowledgeable' (Zaydun ‘alimun). Next, the author distinguishes affirmative (mujiba) propositions, where the subject is correctly said to be the predicate (e.g., 'Man is an animal'), from negative (saliba) propositions, where the subject is correctly said not to be the predicate (e.g., 'Man is not a stone'). The subject may be a specific individual (shakhs mu‘ayyan), making the proposition particular or individual (makhsusa/shakhsiyya). If the subject is universal (kulli) and the scope of its individuals is specified, the proposition is called quantified (mahsuma) and determined (musawwara), and the word indicating the quantity is a quantifier (sur). The quantifiers are listed: for universal affirmative, 'kull' (e.g., 'Every fire is hot'); for universal negative, 'la shay’' and 'la wahid' (e.g., 'No person is inanimate'); for particular affirmative, 'ba‘d' or 'wahid' (e.g., 'Some animal is human'); for particular negative, 'laysa kull', 'laysa ba‘d', or 'ba‘d laysa'. Finally, if the proposition is not explicitly quantified, the author distinguishes two types: natural (tabi‘iyya)—which cannot be intended as universal or particular (e.g., 'The animal is genus'), and indeterminate (muhmala)—which can, like 'Man is at loss'. The indeterminate is logically equivalent to a particular proposition, because from its truth the truth of the particular follows. This classification is foundational for syllogistic logic.

Key Terms

رابطة

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition always contains a subject, a predicate, and a relation (copula), whether explicit or implicit.

Affirmative and Negative Propositions
Definition of affirmative and negative categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a categorical proposition is affirmative or negative based on the relation between subject and predicate.

Core Idea
Affirmation affirms the predicate of the subject; negation denies it.

This passage defines when a proposition is called affirmative or negative. Understanding this distinction is fundamental for constructing valid arguments, as negation affects the truth conditions of propositions in syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
State the proposition
Write down the proposition clearly. For example: 'Man is an animal' or 'Man is not a stone'.
Use the examples from the passage.
2
Check the relation
Determine if the proposition asserts that the subject is the predicate (affirmative) or that the subject is not the predicate (negative). Look for a negation particle like 'not' (laysa).
If there is no negation, it is affirmative.
3
Apply the criterion
If it is correct to say 'the subject is the predicate', it is affirmative. If it is correct to say 'the subject is not the predicate', it is negative. For example, 'Man is an animal' is affirmative because man truly is an animal. 'Man is not a stone' is negative because man is truly not a stone.
Truth is the criterion, not linguistic form.

Quantified Propositions and Their Quantifiers
Classification of quantified propositions and their quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier of a quantified proposition and classify it as universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative.

Core Idea
Quantifiers like 'every' and 'some' turn a universal subject into a precise logical tool.

When the subject of a categorical proposition is universal (kulli), the proposition may be quantified by specifying the scope of the subject. The passage lists the quantifiers (sawar) for each of the four standard types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. This classification is the basis for the square of opposition and syllogistic validity.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the subject is universal
Check if the subject term is a universal (e.g., 'animal', 'man') rather than a specific individual (e.g., 'Zayd'). Only universal subjects can be quantified.
If the subject is individual, the proposition is called shakhsiyya (individual).
2
See if a quantifier is present
Look for words like 'kull' (every), 'la shay'' (nothing), 'ba'd' (some), 'laysa kull' (not every), etc. These are quantifiers (sawar).
If no quantifier, it may be natural or indeterminate (later lesson).
3
Classify the proposition into one of four types
Use the quantifier and the presence/absence of negation: if quantifier 'kull' and no negation => universal affirmative. If 'la shay'' or 'la wahid' => universal negative. If 'ba'd' or 'wahid' => particular affirmative. If 'laysa kull', 'laysa ba'd', or 'ba'd laysa' => particular negative.
Examples from passage: 'Every fire is hot' (universal affirmative), 'No person is inanimate' (universal negative), 'Some animal is human' (particular affirmative).

Propositions

Natural vs Indeterminate Propositions
Distinction between natural and indeterminate propositions, and the logical force of the indeterminate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish an indeterminate proposition (muhmala) from a natural one (tabi'iyya) and explain why the indeterminate is treated as equivalent to a particular proposition.

Core Idea
An indeterminate proposition is logically equivalent to a particular proposition.

Not all propositions have an explicit quantifier. The passage discusses two types of unquantified propositions: natural (tabi'iyya), which cannot be intended as universal or particular, and indeterminate (muhmala), which can. Importantly, the indeterminate is considered equivalent in force to a particular proposition, because if the indeterminate is true, the particular is also true, and vice versa.

Guided Path
1
Check if the proposition has a quantifier
If not, it is either natural or indeterminate. Determine if the proposition can be meaningfully taken as universal or particular.
Example: 'The animal is genus' (tabi'iyya) cannot be intended as 'Every animal is genus' or 'Some animal is genus' because genus is not a predicate that applies to individuals.
2
Classify as natural or indeterminate
If the proposition is not suitable to be interpreted as universal or particular (e.g., because the predicate only applies to the whole kind), it is natural (tabi'iyya). Otherwise, it is indeterminate (muhmala), like 'Man is at loss'.
The passage uses 'Man is at loss' (إِنَّ الْإِنسَانَ لَفِي خُسْرٍ) as an example.
3
Apply the equivalence rule
For indeterminate propositions, the rule is: they are equivalent to particulars. If the indeterminate is true, the corresponding particular is true. For example, if 'Man is at loss' is true, then 'Some man is at loss' is also true.
This equivalence holds because the truth of the indeterminate implies the existence of at least one individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحملية يتحقق بأجزاء ثلاثة موضوع أعني: محكوما عليه ومحمول أعني: محكوما به ونسبة بينهما وتسمى نسبة حكمية، واللفظ الدال عليها يسمى رابطة كهو في زيد هو عالم وتسمى حينئذ ثلاثية، وقد تحذف الرابطة في بعض اللغات وتسمى حينئذ ثنائية كزيد عالم. وهي موجبة إن صح أن يقال: إن الموضوع محمول، كقولنا الإنسان حيوان وسالبة إن صح أن يقال: إن الموضوع ليس بمحمول، كقولنا الإنسان ليس بحجر. فموضوعها إن كان شخصا معينا سميت مخصوصة وشخصية، وإن كان كليا فإن بين فيها مقدار أفراد الموضوع سميت محصورة ومسورة، واللفظ الدال عليه يسمى سورا، وهي إما موجبة كلية وسورها (كل) كقولنا كل نار حارة، أو سالبة كلية وسورها (لا شيء) و (لا) (واحد) كقولنا : لا شيء ولا واحد من الناس بجماد، أو موجبة جزئية، وسورها (بعض) و (واحد) كقولنا بعض الحيوان أو واحد منه إنسان، وسالبة جزئية، وسورها (ليس كل) و(ليس بعض) و(بعض ليس). وإن لم يبين فإن لم تصلح القضية لأن تقصد بها كلية وجزئية سميت طبيعية، وإلا فمهملة كقولنا الحيوان جنس والإنسان لفي خسر، والمهملة في قوة الجزئية؛ إذ متى صدق الإنسان لفي خسر، صدق بعض الإنسان لفي خسر، وبالعكس.
The categorical proposition is realized with three parts: a subject, meaning that which is judged upon; a predicate, meaning that which is judged with; and a relation between them, called a judgmental relation. The word indicating it is called a copula, like 'is' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable', and then it is called tripartite. The copula may be omitted in some languages and then called bipartite, like 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. It is affirmative if it is correct to say that the subject is the predicate, like 'Man is an animal'; and negative if it is correct to say that the subject is not the predicate, like 'Man is not a stone'. If its subject is a specific individual, it is called particular or individual; if it is universal, then if the scope of the individuals of the subject is specified, it is called quantified and determined, and the word indicating it is called a quantifier. It is either universal affirmative with its quantifier 'every' like 'Every fire is hot'; or universal negative with its quantifier 'nothing' and 'no' and 'none' like 'Nothing and no one of people is inanimate'; or particular affirmative with its quantifier 'some' and 'one' like 'Some animal or one of them is human'; and particular negative with its quantifier 'not every' and 'not some' and 'some not'. And if it is not specified, then if the proposition is not suitable to be intended as universal or particular, it is called natural; otherwise it is indeterminate, like 'The animal is genus' and 'Man is indeed at loss'. The indeterminate is in force of the particular, because whenever 'Man is at loss' is true, 'Some man is at loss' is true, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya) and its classification. The author begins by stating that a categorical proposition consists of three essential parts: the subject (mawdu‘), the predicate (mahmul), and the relation (nisba) between them, which is called a judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya). The word that expresses this relation is the copula (rabita), e.g., 'is' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. When the copula is explicit, the proposition is called tripartite; when omitted (as in Arabic often), it is called bipartite, e.g., 'Zayd is knowledgeable' (Zaydun ‘alimun). Next, the author distinguishes affirmative (mujiba) propositions, where the subject is correctly said to be the predicate (e.g., 'Man is an animal'), from negative (saliba) propositions, where the subject is correctly said not to be the predicate (e.g., 'Man is not a stone'). The subject may be a specific individual (shakhs mu‘ayyan), making the proposition particular or individual (makhsusa/shakhsiyya). If the subject is universal (kulli) and the scope of its individuals is specified, the proposition is called quantified (mahsuma) and determined (musawwara), and the word indicating the quantity is a quantifier (sur). The quantifiers are listed: for universal affirmative, 'kull' (e.g., 'Every fire is hot'); for universal negative, 'la shay’' and 'la wahid' (e.g., 'No person is inanimate'); for particular affirmative, 'ba‘d' or 'wahid' (e.g., 'Some animal is human'); for particular negative, 'laysa kull', 'laysa ba‘d', or 'ba‘d laysa'. Finally, if the proposition is not explicitly quantified, the author distinguishes two types: natural (tabi‘iyya)—which cannot be intended as universal or particular (e.g., 'The animal is genus'), and indeterminate (muhmala)—which can, like 'Man is at loss'. The indeterminate is logically equivalent to a particular proposition, because from its truth the truth of the particular follows. This classification is foundational for syllogistic logic.

Key Takeaway
An indeterminate proposition (muhmala) is logically equivalent to a particular proposition in both truth and logical operations.

Classification of Propositions: Modified, Acquired, Simple
Author defines three categories of propositions based on negation location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a proposition as modified (ma‘dula), acquired (muhassala), or simple (basita) based on the location of the negation particle.

Core Idea
The negation's location inside the terms determines modification, not the proposition's truth value.

In this passage, the author introduces a threefold classification of categorical propositions based on whether the negation particle is part of the subject or predicate. This classification is crucial for understanding how logicians handle negated terms and for distinguishing the logical form from the grammatical form.

Guided Path
1
Locate the negation particle
Find the word for 'not' or 'non-' (e.g., la, ghayr, laysa) in the proposition.
The negation particle may be part of the subject, predicate, both, or neither.
2
Check if attached to subject
If the subject term contains a negation (e.g., 'non-living'), it is a candidate for modification.
3
Check if attached to predicate
Similarly, if the predicate term contains a negation, it is also a candidate for modification.
4
Determine if neither term contains negation
If neither subject nor predicate has a negation particle, the proposition is either acquired (if affirmative) or simple (if negative).
5
Classify the proposition
Apply the definitions: if negation is part of a term, it is ma‘dula; if no negation in terms and affirmative, it is muhassala; if no negation in terms and negative, it is basita.
Remember that the overall affirmation/negation depends on the relation, not the terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العدول والتحصيل: حرف السلب إن كان جزءًا من الموضوع فقط، كقولنا: اللاحي جماد، أو من المحمول فقط، كقولنا: الجماد لاحي، أو منهما كقولنا: اللاحي لا عالم سميت معدولة موجبة كانت أو سالبة، وإن لم يكن جزءا لشيء منهما سميت محصلة إن كانت موجبة وبسيطة إن كانت سالبة، والاعتبار بالإيجاب والسلب بالنسبة لا بطرفيها فإن كل ما ليس بحي فهو لا عالم موجبة مع أن طرفيها عدميان، ولا شيء من المتحرك بساكن سالبة مع أن طرفيها وجوديان. والفرق بين البسيطة والموجبة المعدولة المحمول في اللفظ، أما في الثلاثية فبأنها موجبة معدولة إن قدمت الرابطة على حرف السلب، وسالبة بسيطة إن رتب عنها، وأما في الثنائية فبالنية، أو بالاصطلاح على تخصيص لفظ غير أو لا بالإيجاب المعدول، ولفظ ليس بالسلب البسيط، أو بالعكس.
Section on modification and acquisition: If the negation particle is a part of the subject only, like our saying 'the non-living is inanimate', or of the predicate only, like our saying 'the inanimate is non-living', or of both, like our saying 'the non-living is not knowledgeable', it is called modified, whether affirmative or negative. And if it is not a part of either of them, it is called acquired if affirmative, and simple if negative. The consideration is with affirmation and negation in relation to the relation, not to its two ends. For 'everything that is not living is not knowledgeable' is affirmative even though its two ends are negative, and 'nothing that is moving is stationary' is negative even though its two ends are positive. The difference between the simple and the modified affirmative predicate in wording: as for the tripartite, it is a modified affirmative if the copula precedes the negation particle, and a simple negative if it is placed after it. As for the bipartite, it is by intention, or by convention to assign the word 'ghayr' or 'la' to the modified affirmative, and the word 'laysa' to the simple negative, or vice versa.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions based on where the negation particle (harf al-salb) appears relative to the subject and predicate. The author defines 'modified' (ma‘dula) as a proposition where the negation is part of the subject (e.g., 'the non-living is inanimate'), part of the predicate (e.g., 'the inanimate is non-living'), or both (e.g., 'the non-living is not knowledgeable'). This modification applies to both affirmative and negative propositions. If the negation particle is not part of either term, it is called 'acquired' (muhassala) if affirmative, and 'simple' (basita) if negative. The key point is that the classification of a proposition as affirmative or negative depends solely on the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate, not on the ontological status of the terms themselves. For example, 'Everything that is not living is not knowledgeable' (kull ma laysa bi-hayy fa-huwa la ‘alim) is affirmative because the relation is affirmative, even though both subject and predicate contain a negation. Conversely, 'Nothing that is moving is stationary' (la shay’ min al-mutaharrik bi-sakin) is negative despite both terms being positive. The author then explains how to distinguish between a simple negative and a modified affirmative in wording. In tripartite propositions (with explicit copula), if the copula comes before the negation particle, it indicates a modified affirmative (e.g., 'Zayd is not-writing' as a positive statement about non-writing). If the negation particle comes before the copula, it is a simple negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not a writer'). In bipartite propositions (without copula), the distinction relies on the intention of the speaker or on a terminological convention where words like 'ghayr' or 'la' are used for modified affirmatives, and 'laysa' for simple negatives, or vice versa depending on the agreed convention. This section is important for understanding how logicians handle negative terms and the logical form of propositions.

Distinguishing Modified Affirmative from Simple Negative
Author explains how to differentiate modified affirmatives from simple negatives in wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a modified affirmative proposition from a simple negative proposition based on word order in tripartite and bipartite propositions.

Core Idea
In tripartite propositions, the copula-negation order resolves the ambiguity; in bipartite, it relies on convention.

The passage also provides practical rules to tell apart a modified affirmative (e.g., 'Zayd is not-writing') from a simple negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not a writer'). In tripartite propositions with an explicit copula, the position of the negation particle relative to the copula determines the classification. In bipartite propositions, where no copula appears, the distinction depends on the speaker's intention or a terminological convention.

Guided Path
1
Identify proposition type (tripartite or bipartite)
Check if the proposition has an explicit copula (e.g., huwa, kana) or not.
2
For tripartite: locate copula and negation
If the copula precedes the negation particle (e.g., Zayd huwa la katib), it is a modified affirmative. If the negation precedes the copula (e.g., Zayd laysa bi-katib), it is a simple negative.
This is explicitly given in the passage.
3
For bipartite: rely on convention or intention
Since no copula appears, the distinction is made by assigning certain words to each type. For example, 'ghayr' or 'la' may indicate modified affirmative, while 'laysa' indicates simple negative, or vice versa.
The author mentions 'or the opposite', indicating it is a convention.
4
Apply the rule
Classify sample propositions by checking the criteria above.

Classification of Modal Propositions: Simple and Composite
Author begins classification of modal propositions: thirteen types, simple and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify modal propositions into simple and composite based on the author's definition and identify the total number of types.

Core Idea
Thirteen modal propositions, six simple and seven composite, form the backbone of modal logic.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of modal propositions (al-qadaya al-muwajjaha) according to the logician's convention. The author states that there are exactly thirteen such propositions, divided into two main categories: simple (basīṭa) and composite (murakkaba). Simple modal propositions are those whose modality involves only affirmation or negation, not a combination of modalities. Understanding this division is the first step toward mastering modal logic, which is essential for evaluating arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage is from a chapter on modal propositions. Modal propositions are those that specify a modality (e.g., necessity, possibility) alongside the subject-predicate relation.
2
Note the total number
The author states that the modal propositions investigated are thirteen in total. This is a conventional number agreed upon by logicians.
The number may vary slightly among different logicians, but thirteen is standard.
3
Distinguish simple from composite
The thirteen are divided into two groups: simple and composite. Simple ones have a modality that is either purely affirmative or purely negative (e.g., 'necessarily' alone). Composite ones involve a combination (e.g., 'necessary and possible').
The definition of simple is given: 'those whose reality is only affirmation or only negation'.
4
Recognize the incompleteness
The text ends after stating that the simple ones are six. The actual list of the six simple types is missing due to page break. This lesson focuses only on the classification framework.
In subsequent pages, the six types (e.g., necessary, absolute, possible) would be enumerated.

Absolute Necessary and Absolute Permanent Propositions
Definitions of the absolute necessary and absolute permanent modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة) and absolute permanent (دائمة مطلقة) modal propositions and identify their defining features.

Core Idea
Necessity is stronger than permanence; permanence only requires continuity, not logical inevitability.

In this lesson, we examine the first two simple modal propositions from the author's list: the absolute necessary and the absolute permanent. Both concern the duration of the subject's existence, but they differ in modal strength. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common feature
Both the absolute necessary and absolute permanent propositions share the condition 'as long as the essence of the subject exists' (ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودا). Note that the judgment holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence.
This condition ties the truth to the subject's existence, not to any temporary description.
2
Extract the differentiae
For the absolute necessary, the judgment is made with necessity (ضرورة). For the absolute permanent, it is made with perpetuity (دوام) without necessity. The first implies that the predicate cannot be otherwise; the second only that it is always the case.
The author's example for necessary: 'Every human is necessarily an animal.' For permanent, he refers to a previous example.
3
Contrast the examples
Consider: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' (ضرورية مطلقة) vs. 'Every human is perpetually breathing' (if such were true, it would be دائمة مطلقة). The first is necessary because being animal is essential; the second is only perpetual if humans always breathe, but it is not necessary since breathing can stop.
The permanent proposition does not assert that the predicate cannot cease; it only asserts that it never ceases as long as the subject exists.
4
Apply the distinction to a new case
Take the proposition 'Every Muslim is necessarily a believer.' Is it necessary or permanent? It is necessary if being Muslim entails belief (essential). If it only happens that all Muslims are believers but could be otherwise, it would be permanent.
This exercise trains you to check whether the predicate is essential or accidental.

فصل في القضايا الموجهة التي جرى الاصطلاح بالبحث عنها ثلاث عشرة، بسيطة ومركبة. أ-أما البسائط - وهي التي حقيقتها إيجاب فقط أو سلب فقط- فستة: 1 - الضرورية المطلقة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودا، كقولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان حيوان وبالضرورة لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر. 2 - الدائمة المطلقة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه ما دام ذاته موجودا، وقد مر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا. 3 - المشروطة العامة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه بشرط وصف الموضوع كقولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا، وبالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا. 4 - العرفية العامة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه بشرط وصفه ومر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا. 5 - المطلقة العامة وهي التي يحكم فيها بثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه بالفعل، كقولنا : بالإطلاق العام كل إنسان متنفس، وبه لا شيء من الإنسان بمتنفس. 6 - الممكنة العامة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة المطلقة من الجانب المخالف للحكم، كقولنا بالإمكان العام كل نار حارة، وبه لا شيء من الحار ببارد.
Chapter on modal propositions which it has been conventional to investigate thirteen, simple and compound. a) As for the simple ones—which are whose essence is only affirmation or only negation—are six: 1. The absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa): that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it as long as the essence of the subject exists, like our saying 'Necessarily, every human is an animal' and 'Necessarily, nothing of human is a stone.' 2. The absolute permanent (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa): that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it as long as its essence exists; its example in affirmation and negation has already been given. 3. The general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma): that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it under the condition of the description of the subject, like our saying 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' and 'Necessarily, nothing of the writer is motionless of fingers as long as he is a writer.' 4. The general customary (al-'urfiya al-'amma): that in which judgment is made of the perpetuity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it under the condition of its description; its example in affirmation and negation has already been given. 5. The general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma): that in which judgment is made of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it in actuality, like our saying 'By absolute universal, every human is breathing' and 'By it, nothing of human is breathing.' 6. The general possible (al-mumkina al-'amma): that in which judgment is made of the removal of absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment, like our saying 'By universal possibility, every fire is hot' and 'By it, nothing of hot is cold.'
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of modal propositions, stating that there are thirteen types (simple and compound) but only the six simple ones are defined here. The author lists each with a precise definition and example. The first is 'absolute necessary' (ضرورية مطلقة), which asserts that the predicate is necessarily true or false of the subject as long as the subject exists. Example: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' and 'No human is necessarily a stone.' The second is 'absolute permanent' (دائمة مطلقة), which asserts perpetuity (not necessity) of the predicate for the subject as long as the subject exists. An example has already been given (presumably earlier in the text). The third is 'general conditional' (مشروطة عامة), which asserts necessity conditional on the subject having a certain description (e.g., being a writer). Example: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' The fourth is 'general customary' (عرفية عامة), which asserts perpetuity conditional on the subject's description. The fifth is 'general absolute' (مطلقة عامة), which asserts actual (non-modal) truth as in 'Every human is actually breathing.' The sixth is 'general possible' (ممكنة عامة), which asserts the removal of necessity from the contradictory side, meaning the proposition is not necessary but possible. Example: 'Every fire is possibly hot' meaning it is not necessary that fire cannot be hot, i.e., it is possible. These six categories form the foundation of modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
ضرورية مطلقة
دائمة مطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the absolute necessary proposition (ضرورية مطلقة) from the absolute permanent proposition (دائمة مطلقة)?
A) The absolute necessary includes a condition on the subject's description; the absolute permanent does not.
B) The absolute necessary asserts necessity; the absolute permanent asserts perpetuity only.
C) The absolute necessary applies only to non-physical subjects; the absolute permanent applies to physical subjects.
D) The absolute necessary is used only in negative propositions; the absolute permanent is used only in affirmative ones.

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary and absolute permanent both hold 'as long as the subject exists', but necessity implies logical inevitability, while permanence implies only continuous actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following proposition as absolute necessary or absolute permanent, and justify: 'Every human is necessarily a living being.' (Answer: necessary, because 'living' is part of human essence.)
?
Construct an example of an absolute permanent proposition using an Islamic science example (e.g., from Fiqh or Kalam) and explain why it is not necessary.

General Conditional and General Customary Propositions
Definitions of the general conditional and general customary modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate between the general conditional (مشروطة عامة) and general customary (عرفية عامة) modal propositions based on their definitions and examples.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions add a description condition; necessity/permanence is then relative to that condition.

The third and fourth simple modal propositions introduce a conditional element: the judgment is made under the condition of the subject having a certain description (e.g., being a writer). The general conditional asserts necessity under that condition, while the general customary asserts perpetuity. This lesson unpacks both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional clause
Both propositions have the condition 'under the condition of the description of the subject' (بشرط وصف الموضوع). The judgment holds only when the subject is described by that attribute.
The condition is part of the modality: the truth is conditional on the description.
2
Distinguish necessity vs. perpetuity
The general conditional (مشروطة عامة) judges with necessity (ضرورة) under the description. The general customary (عرفية عامة) judges with perpetuity (دوام) under the description. Example for conditional: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' This is necessary because moving fingers is essential to writing. For customary, a perpetual example might be 'Every writer is perpetually writing as long as he is a writer' (if writing is defined as the act, but it's not necessary because one could pause).
The author notes that the example for customary has already been given; we can infer it follows the same pattern.
3
Contrast with unconditional modal propositions
Unlike the absolute necessary/permanent, these propositions do not require the predicate to hold for the entire existence of the subject, but only when the subject possesses a specific description. For instance, a person is not always a writer; they are a writer only at certain times.
This restriction makes these propositions weaker in scope than the first two.
4
Apply to a new example
Consider: 'Every mujtahid is necessarily correct as long as he is a mujtahid.' Is this a general conditional? Yes, because it asserts necessity under the condition of being a mujtahid. If it were only perpetually correct (but could err), it would be general customary.
This exercise helps in recognizing the modal force.

General Absolute and General Possible Propositions
Definitions of the general absolute and general possible modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and differentiate the general absolute (مطلقة عامة) and general possible (ممكنة عامة) modal propositions, and recognize their roles in non-modal and possibility contexts.

Core Idea
Absolute propositions assert actuality; possible propositions assert the absence of necessity in the opposite.

The last two simple modal propositions are the general absolute and the general possible. The general absolute is actually a non-modal proposition that simply affirms or denies a predicate in actuality. The general possible introduces possibility by negating the necessity of the contradictory. This lesson clarifies both.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general absolute
The general absolute (مطلقة عامة) is a simple categorical proposition without any modal force: it judges the predicate as actually (بالفعل) holding or not holding for the subject. It does not assert necessity or perpetuity. Example: 'By absolute universal, every human is breathing.' This merely reports a fact without modal qualification.
This is the baseline proposition from which modal propositions are derived.
2
Understand the general possible
The general possible (ممكنة عامة) asserts that the opposite of the judgment is not necessary. In other words, it is possible that the predicate holds, because there is no necessity preventing it. Example: 'By universal possibility, every fire is hot.' This means it is not necessary that fire is not hot; it is possible for fire to be hot.
The definition: 'removal of absolute necessity from the side opposite to the judgment' (ارتفاع الضرورة المطلقة من الجانب المخالف للحكم).
3
Contrast the two
The general absolute says something is actually the case. The general possible says something could be the case (its opposite is not necessary). They are not contradictories: a general absolute proposition implies its general possible counterpart (if it is actually true, then it is possible), but not vice versa.
For example, 'Every human is actually breathing' implies 'Every human is possibly breathing', but the reverse does not hold.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider: 'Every Muslim is actually praying five times a day' (general absolute, but false). The possible version: 'Every Muslim is possibly praying five times a day' (true, because it is not necessary that they do not pray). The possible proposition is weaker.
This shows the utility of possible propositions in modal logic for expressing contingency.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Nature of Composite Modal Propositions
Definition of composite proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two defining characteristics of a composite proposition and determine its overall quality from its first component.

Core Idea
A composite proposition merges two opposite-quality propositions of the same quantity into one.

In logical analysis, propositions can be simple or composite. A composite proposition is not a mere conjunction but a single unified judgment formed from two simpler propositions. The author defines the composite proposition and introduces the concept that its quality is taken from the first component. Understanding this is essential before exploring the seven types of composites.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of composite proposition
Find the Arabic phrase that defines the composite proposition. It begins with 'والمركبة - وهي التي حقيقتها يتركب من قضيتين مخالفتي الكيفية موافقتي الكمية'.
2
Identify the two components
Note that they must be opposite in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and agree in quantity (both universal or both particular).
3
Determine how the composite's quality is decided
The text says 'معتبرا إيجابها وسلبها بالقضية الأولى' meaning the overall quality is taken from the first proposition.
4
Apply the definition to a simple example
For instance, consider a composite that is affirmative if the first is affirmative. This will be used later in specific types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - والمركبة - وهي التي حقيقتها يتركب من قضيتين مخالفتي الكيفية موافقتي الكمية، معتبرا إيجابها وسلبها بالقضية الأولى - فسبع: 1 - المشروطة الخاصة، وهي المشروطة العامة مع اللادوام بحسب الذات واللادوام عبارة عن مطلقة عامة، فالمشروطة الخاصة إن كانت موجبة كقولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما، فتركيبها من موجبة مشروطة عامة وسالبة مطلقة عامة، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما فمن سالبة مشروطة عامة، وموجبة مطلقة عامة. 2 - العرفية الخاصة، وهي العرفية العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات، وهي إن كانت موجبة فتركيبها من موجبة عرفية عامة وسالبة مطلقة عامة، وإن كانت سالبة فتركيبها من سالبة عرفية عامة وموجبة مطلقة عامة، ومثالها قد مر . 3 - الوجودية اللاضرورية وهي المطلقة العامة مع قيد اللاضرورة بحسب الذات، واللاضرورة عبارة عن ممكنة عامة، فالوجودية اللاضرورية إن كانت موجبة كقولنا كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة، فمن موجبة مطلقة عامة، وسالبة ممكنة عامة، وإن كانت سالبة كقولنا : لا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل لا بالضرورة، فمن سالبة مطلقة عامة، وموجبة ممكنة عامة. 4 - الوجودية اللادائمة المطلقة العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات وهي سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة فمن مطلقتين عامتين، إحداهما موجبة، والأخرى سالبة، ومثالها ما مر .
B. The composite: it is that whose reality is composed of two propositions opposite in quality and agreeing in quantity, considering its affirmation and negation according to the first proposition. There are seven: 1. The special conditional: it is the general conditional with the condition of non-permanence with respect to essence, and non-permanence is a general absolute proposition. So the special conditional, if affirmative, like our saying: necessarily every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer not always, then its composition is from an affirmative general conditional and a negative general absolute. And if negative, like our saying: necessarily no writer is a finger-still as long as he is a writer not always, then it is from a negative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute. 2. The special conventional: it is the general conventional with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to essence. If affirmative, its composition is from an affirmative general conventional and a negative general absolute. If negative, its composition is from a negative general conventional and an affirmative general absolute, and its example has passed. 3. The existential non-necessary: it is the general absolute with the restriction of non-necessity with respect to essence, and non-necessity is a general possible proposition. So the existential non-necessary, if affirmative like our saying: every human is actually laughing not necessarily, then it is from an affirmative general absolute and a negative general possible. And if negative like our saying: no human is actually laughing not necessarily, then it is from a negative general absolute and an affirmative general possible. 4. The existential non-permanent: it is the general absolute with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to essence. Whether affirmative or negative, it is from two general absolutes, one affirmative and the other negative, and its example has passed.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage introduces composite modal propositions (al-murakkaba), which are formed by combining two simple propositions that have opposite quality (one affirmative, one negative) but the same quantity (both universal or both particular). The composite is considered as a single proposition, with its overall quality determined by the first of the two components. The author states there are seven types, but only four are given in this section. 1. The special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa): This is formed from a general conditional plus the condition of non-permanence (i.e., it is not always true during the subject's existence). The non-permanence itself is expressed by a general absolute proposition that denies the predicate at some time. So an affirmative special conditional combines an affirmative general conditional (e.g., 'necessarily every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer') with a negative general absolute (e.g., 'not always' means at some time he is not a finger-mover). The example given is 'necessarily every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer not always'. 2. The special conventional (al-‘urfiyya al-khassa): Similar to the special conditional but using the general conventional instead of conditional. The non-permanence restriction is the same. 3. The existential non-necessary (al-wujudiyya la-daruriyya): This combines a general absolute (actual existence) with the condition of non-necessity (i.e., it is not necessary). Non-necessity is expressed by a general possible proposition. So an affirmative existential non-necessary combines an affirmative general absolute (e.g., 'every human is actually laughing') with a negative general possible (e.g., 'not necessarily' meaning it is possible that he is not laughing). Example: 'every human is actually laughing not necessarily'. 4. The existential non-permanent (al-wujudiyya la-da'ima): This is a general absolute with a restriction of non-permanence. Unlike the others, it is composed of two general absolute propositions, one affirmative and one negative, indicating that the predicate actually holds at some time but not always.

Key Terms
المركبة (al-murakkaba): A proposition composed of two simple propositions opposite in quality and agreeing in quantity, with its overall judgment considered from the first proposition.
قضية (qadiyya): A proposition or judgment.
كيفية (kayfiyya): Quality (affirmative or negative) of a proposition.
كمية (kammiyya): Quantity (universal or particular) of a proposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What determines the overall quality (affirmative/negative) of a composite proposition?
A) The quality of the second proposition
B) The quantity of the propositions
C) The quality of the first proposition
D) The modality of the components

Key Takeaway
A composite proposition is defined by two components opposite in quality, same quantity, and its quality is taken from the first.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the definition of composite proposition, construct a composite proposition from two simple propositions about the attributes of Allah (e.g., 'Allah is knowing' and 'Allah is not ignorant') and state its overall quality.
?
Identify whether the following is a composite: 'Every human is mortal and no human is immortal.' Explain why.

The Special Conditional (al-Mashruta al-Khassa)
Definition and composition of the special conditional composite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the composition of a special conditional (affirmative or negative) from its components.

Core Idea
The special conditional adds non-permanence to the general conditional using a general absolute.

The author now defines the first type of composite: the special conditional. It is derived from the general conditional by adding the condition of non-permanence (that the predicate does not hold at all times of the subject's existence). This is a crucial modality for expressing temporal restrictions in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base proposition
The special conditional starts from the general conditional (المشروطة العامة), which asserts a necessary connection.
2
Add the non-permanence condition
Non-permanence (اللادوام) is expressed as a general absolute proposition that denies the predicate at some time. For affirmative, this is a negative general absolute; for negative, it is affirmative.
3
Determine the composition for affirmative and negative cases
For affirmative: use an affirmative general conditional + a negative general absolute. For negative: use a negative general conditional + an affirmative general absolute.
4
Test with the given example
Example: 'necessarily every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer not always'. The first component (affirmative general conditional) asserts necessity during the writing state; the second (negative general absolute) denies that this is always true.

Source Text

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة (al-mashruta al-khassa): A composite proposition derived from the general conditional with the addition of non-permanence with respect to the subject's essence.
المشروطة العامة (al-mashruta al-‘amma): General conditional proposition: e.g., 'necessarily every A is B as long as it exists'.
اللادوام (al-la-dawam): Non-permanence: the condition that the predicate does not hold at all times during the subject's existence.
مطلقة عامة (mutlaqa ‘amma): General absolute proposition: e.g., 'every A is B' without any temporal or modal qualification.

Key Takeaway
The special conditional is composed of a general conditional plus a negative general absolute (for affirmative) or plus an affirmative general absolute (for negative).

The Special Conventional (al-'Urfiyya al-Khassa)
Definition of special conventional composite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the composition of a special conventional proposition and contrast it with the special conditional.

Core Idea
The special conventional is like the special conditional but based on the conventional (usual) rather than conditional necessity.

The second type of composite is the special conventional. It closely parallels the special conditional, but instead of using a general conditional as the base, it uses a general conventional proposition. The non-permanence condition is identical. This distinction is important for distinguishing between necessary and habitual connections in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base proposition
The special conventional starts from the general conventional (العرفية العامة), which expresses what is usual or customary.
2
Add the non-permanence condition
As with the special conditional, non-permanence is represented by a general absolute proposition.
3
Determine the composition for affirmative and negative cases
For affirmative: affirmative general conventional + negative general absolute. For negative: negative general conventional + affirmative general absolute.
4
Compare with the special conditional
The only difference is the base: 'conditional' vs 'conventional'. The non-permanence condition and the composition pattern are the same.

The Existential Non-Necessary (al-Wujudiyya la-Daruriyya)
Definition of existential non-necessary composite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the composition of an existential non-necessary proposition and recognize its use of possibility.

Core Idea
The existential non-necessary combines actual existence with the possibility of non-existence.

The third type of composite is the existential non-necessary. Unlike the previous two, this composite uses a general absolute as its base and adds the condition of non-necessity, which is expressed by a general possible proposition. This type is crucial for propositions that assert actuality while denying necessity, common in discussions of contingency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base proposition
The base is the general absolute (المطلقة العامة), which asserts actual existence without any modal qualification.
2
Add the non-necessity condition
Non-necessity (اللاضرورة) is defined as a general possible proposition (ممكنة عامة), which states that the opposite is possible.
3
Determine the composition for affirmative and negative cases
For affirmative: affirmative general absolute + negative general possible. For negative: negative general absolute + affirmative general possible.
4
Apply to the given example
Example: 'every human is actually laughing not necessarily'. The first component (affirmative general absolute) affirms actual laughter; the second (negative general possible) denies necessity, i.e., it is possible that a human is not laughing.
