Parts of a Categorical Proposition: Subject, Predicate, and Relation
Author enumerates the three necessary parts of a categorical proposition and discusses the copula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three parts of a categorical proposition (subject, predicate, judgmental relation) and distinguish the copula as the word indicating that relation.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition is built from subject, predicate, and the judgmental relation — the copula makes the relation explicit.

In this passage, the author breaks down the categorical proposition into its essential components. Understanding these three parts is crucial for analysing any logical statement. We will focus on identifying the subject, predicate, and the judgmental relation, and see how the copula (like 'kana' or 'huwa') points to that relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject (mawdu')
Find the term about which something is being affirmed or denied. For example, in 'Zayd is standing', the subject is 'Zayd'.
The subject is the 'judged upon' part.
2
Identify the predicate (mahmul)
Find the term that is being affirmed or denied of the subject. In the same example, 'standing' is the predicate.
The predicate is the 'judged by' part.
3
Identify the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya)
Determine whether the proposition is affirmative or negative. This is not a separate word but the affirmation/negation itself.
The relation is what makes it a judgment.
4
Find the copula (rabita) if present
Look for a word that explicitly indicates the relation, such as 'kana' (was/is) or 'huwa' (he). In 'Zayd huwa 'alim', 'huwa' serves as copula.
Sometimes the copula is omitted in Arabic, as in 'Zayd kātib'.
5
Distinguish the copula from other uses of the same word
Be aware that words like 'huwa' can also be pronouns. The author discusses the controversy: 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa qā'im' is not a true copula but a pronoun. Only consider it a copula when it explicitly indicates the relation.
This nuance prevents confusion in logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من تقسيم القضية إلى الحملية والشرطية شرع الآن في بيان أجزاء الحملية وأقسامها ولما كانت الحملية من الشرطية بمنزلة المفرد من المركب قدم الحملية فقال: (و) القضية (الحملية إنما تتحقق بأجزاء ثلاثة): أحدها (موضوع أعني محكوما عليه كزيد في زيد قائم» (و) ثانيها: (محمول أعني محكوما به كقائم في المثال المذكور، (و) ثالثها: (نسبة بينهما) أي: بين الموضوع والمحمول، (وتسمى نسبة حكمية و اللفظ الدال عليها يسمى رابطة)؛ لارتباطه المحمول بالموضوع، وهي قد تكون في صيغة الكلمة كـ«كان» في قوله تعالى : وكان الله عليما حكيما [النساء: ۸۰] ، وقد تكون في صيغة الاسم (ك«هو» في زيد هو عالم). والمراد بالنسبة الحكمية الإيجاب والسلب لا النسبة التي هي موردهما، ولا حاجة إلى اللفظ الدال على النسبة التي هي موردهما؛ لأن اللفظ الدال عليهمـا دال عليهـا، فالجزءان(۱) من القضية يعبران بعبارة واحدة فصار كجزء واحد من القضية، فانحصرت الأجزاء في الثلاثة وإلا فأجزاء القضية أربعة لا ثلاثة. وفيه بحث؛ لأن لفظ «هو» و «هي» ونحوهما ضمائر وضعت لما تقدم ذكره عليها، ولا دلالة لها على النسبة أصلا، وإنما تدل على ما تقدم؛ إذ ليس مدلول «هو» في قولنا: «زيد هو قائم» إلا زيد، فلا يكون رابطة، وإن قيل: إن «هو» في المثال المذكور ضمير فصل قلنا على تقدير التسليم: إن ضمير الفصل لا يدل على النسبة الحكمية، بل على الفرق، ويمكن أن يجاب بأن «هو» ههنا ليس بمستعمل للكناية، بل استعمل مكانَ «هست» في الفارسية، هكذا صرَّح الحكيم المحقق، والفيلسوف المدقق. ولما فرغ عن بيان أجزاء الحملية شرع في تقسيمها باعتبار الرابطة فقال: (وتسمى) القضية (حينئذ ) أي : حين إذا ذكرت فيها الرابطة (ثلاثية)؛ لاشتمالها على ثلاثة ألفاظ، وقد يُحذف الرابطة في بعض اللغات) أي: لغة العرب بشهادة القرائن الدالة عليها؛ إذ يقولون : زيد كاتب.
When he finished dividing the proposition into categorical and conditional, he now began to explain the parts of the categorical and its divisions. And since the categorical is to the conditional as the single word is to the composite, he presented the categorical first, saying: (And) the categorical proposition is realized by three parts: one of them is the subject, meaning that which is judged upon, like Zayd in 'Zayd is standing'; and the second is the predicate, meaning that which is judged by, like 'standing' in the mentioned example; and the third is the relation between them, i.e., between subject and predicate, and it is called a judgmental relation, and the word indicating it is called a copula (rabita) because it links the predicate to the subject. And it may be in the form of a verb, like 'kana' in His statement: 'And Allah is ever All-Knowing, All-Wise' [Quran 4:80], and it may be in the form of a noun, like 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa knowledgeable'. And what is meant by the judgmental relation is affirmation and negation, not the relation that is their subject matter. And there is no need for the word indicating the relation that is their subject matter, because the word indicating them (i.e., affirmation/negation) indicates it. So the two parts of the proposition are expressed by one phrase, so it becomes like one part of the proposition. Thus the parts are limited to three; otherwise the parts of the proposition would be four, not three. But there is a discussion about this: because the word 'huwa', 'hiya', and their likes are pronouns placed for what was previously mentioned, and they have no indication of the relation at all; they only indicate what precedes. For the meaning of 'huwa' in our saying 'Zayd huwa standing' is nothing but Zayd, so it is not a copula. And if it is said that 'huwa' in the mentioned example is a pronoun of separation (damir fasl), we say, assuming this is granted: the pronoun of separation does not indicate the judgmental relation, but rather indicates distinction. And it can be answered that 'huwa' here is not used for allusion (kinaya), but is used in place of 'hast' in Persian; thus declared the investigating sage and the exacting philosopher. When he finished explaining the parts of the categorical, he began dividing it according to the copula, saying: (And the proposition is called) then, i.e., when the copula is mentioned in it, (tripartite) because it contains three words. And the copula may be omitted in some languages, i.e., the Arabic language, by evidence of indicators indicating it; for they say: 'Zayd is a writer' (without an explicit copula).
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage explains the essential parts of a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). The author states that a categorical proposition is realized by three parts: subject (mawdu'), predicate (mahmul), and a relation (nisba) between them, which is called the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya). The word indicating this relation is called the copula (rabita). Examples are given: the verb 'kana' in the Quranic verse 'And Allah is ever All-Knowing, All-Wise', and the pronoun 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa knowledgeable'. The author clarifies that the judgmental relation is affirmation or negation themselves, not the relation that is their subject matter (i.e., the connection between the two concepts). There is no need for a separate word for that subject-matter relation because the word indicating affirmation/negation already indicates it. Therefore, the two parts (the judgmental relation and its subject matter) are expressed by one phrase, reducing the count to three parts. However, a discussion (bahth) arises: the words 'huwa' and 'hiya' are pronouns that refer back to something previously mentioned, and they do not indicate the relation at all. For example, 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa standing' only refers to Zayd. So it cannot be a copula. If one argues that 'huwa' here is a pronoun of separation (damir fasl), the author responds that even so, it does not indicate the judgmental relation but distinction. A possible answer is that 'huwa' in such constructions is not used as a pronoun but is used in place of the Persian word 'hast' (meaning 'is'), as stated by the investigating sage and exacting philosopher. Finally, the author notes that when the copula is explicit, the proposition is called 'tripartite' (thalathiyya) because it has three words. In Arabic, the copula is often omitted, as in 'Zayd is a writer' (Zayd kātib), because the context provides evidence. This passage is crucial for understanding the structure of categorical propositions, which are the foundation of syllogistic logic.

Key Takeaway
The three parts of a categorical proposition are subject, predicate, and the judgmental relation; the copula is a word indicating that relation, but it is not always present in Arabic.

The Nature of the Copula in Categorical Propositions
Author discusses the copula in categorical propositions, refuting a claim and citing al-Razi and al-Armuwi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different views on the copula (rabit) in categorical propositions and explain why it is not redundant even when the predicate is a derived noun.

Core Idea
The copula specifies the relation to a particular subject, distinct from any implied relation.

In Arabic categorical propositions, the copula linking subject and predicate can be implicit or explicit. Some thought it was merely the inflectional vowel, but Imam al-Razi and al-Armuwi argued that the copula has a distinct function. In this lesson, we dissect the debate and clarify the true nature of the copula.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The passage begins with an objection: some say the copula is the inflectional vowel. Understand this claim and its basis.
The claim arises from Arabic sentences like 'Zayd Katib' where the vowel indicates the predicate relation.
2
Find the author's refutation
The author argues that if the vowel were the copula, then the word 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa qa'im' would be superfluous. Note the logical implication.
3
Analyze al-Razi's argument on binary propositions
Al-Razi says that when the predicate is a derived noun, the relation is implied, so an explicit copula would cause repetition. Understand his reasoning.
Binary propositions (thunaiyya) consist of only subject and predicate, like 'Zayd knowledgeable'.
4
Examine al-Armuwi's response
Al-Armuwi distinguishes between an unspecified relation (implied in the derived noun) and a specific relation (indicated by the copula), so no redundancy occurs.
This resolves the conflict: the derived noun indicates relation to 'some subject', while the copula indicates relation to 'this subject'.
5
Synthesize the correct view
Conclude that the copula is a distinct element that specifies the subject's relation, and its inclusion is not redundant even with derived predicates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قيل: إن الرابطة ههنا هي الحركة الإعرابية؛ لأنَّا إذا قلنا : «زيد كاتب» على سبيل التعداد لم يكن هناك ربط قلت لو كانت الحركة الإعرابية رابطة لكان «هو» في قولنا: «زيد هو «قائم» زائدا على قدر الحاجة، وقيل: يجب ذكر الرابطة في لغة العجم؛ إذ لا يقولون: «زيد نويسنده»، بل زید نویسنده است»، وإنما قال: في بعض اللغات)؛ لعدم العلم بجواز حذفها في جميع اللغات. (وتسمى القضية ( حينئذ ثنائية)؛ لاشتمالها على جزئين كزيد عالم، قال الإمام في الملخص: "القضية التي محمولها كلمة أو اسم مشتق ثنائية في اللفظ ثلاثية بالطبع؛ لأن النسبة مدلول عليها ،تضمنا فذكرها يوجب التكرار ؛ لأنه يصير حينئذ هكذا: «زيد هو يكتب هو»، و«زيد هو كاتب هو»، ولا شك أنه تكرار". وأجاب عنه سراج الملة والدين محمد بن أبي بكر الأرموي بأن الكلمة أو الاسم المشتق دالة على نسبة إلى موضوع ما والرابط دال على النسبة إلى موضوع معين فأين أحدهما عن الآخر؟
If it is said: Indeed the copula here is the inflectional vowel; because when we say 'Zayd is a writer' by way of enumeration there is no link. I say: If the inflectional vowel were a copula then 'huwa' in our saying 'Zayd huwa qa'im' would be superfluous beyond need. And it was said: The copula must be mentioned in the language of the non-Arabs; since they do not say 'Zayd nawisandeh' but rather 'Zayd nawisandeh ast'. And he only said 'in some languages' due to lack of knowledge of the permissibility of omitting it in all languages. (The proposition is then called binary) due to its inclusion of two parts like 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. Al-Imam said in al-Mulakhkhas: 'A proposition whose predicate is a verbal noun or derived noun is binary in wording, ternary by nature; because the relation is implied by inclusion, so mentioning it would cause repetition; because it would then become like: 'Zayd huwa yaktubu huwa', and 'Zayd huwa katibun huwa', and there is no doubt that this is repetition.' And Siraj al-Milla wa al-Din Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr al-Armuwi answered him that the verbal noun or derived noun indicates a relation to some subject, while the copula indicates the relation to a specific subject, so how far is one from the other?
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage contains a dialectical discussion on the nature of the copula (rabit) in categorical propositions. It begins by presenting an objection: some claim that the copula is the inflectional vowel (haraka i'rabiyya), as in the Arabic sentence 'Zayd Katib' where the vowel on the last letter indicates the predicate relation. The author refutes this by arguing that if that were true, then the word 'huwa' (he) in a sentence like 'Zayd huwa qa'im' would be grammatically superfluous, yet it is sometimes used for emphasis. The text then notes that in non-Arabic languages (like Persian), the copula must be explicitly stated because they do not have inflectional endings. The passage then defines the 'binary proposition' (thunaiyya) as one consisting only of subject and predicate, without an explicit copula, exemplified by 'Zayd knowledgeable'. It quotes Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi from his al-Mulakhkhas, who argues that when the predicate is a verbal noun or derived noun, the proposition is binary in wording but ternary in nature because the relation (nisba) is implicitly contained in the predicate, so stating the copula would result in repetition like 'Zayd huwa yaktubu huwa' which is redundant. Al-Armuwi responds that the derived predicate indicates a relation to an unspecified subject, while the copula specifies the relation to a particular subject, so they are not redundant.

قضية ثنائية
مشتق

B) Because the derived noun indicates a relation to some subject, while the copula indicates the relation to a specific subject.
C) Because the derived noun and the copula both indicate the same relation, so one is redundant.
D) Because the copula is only needed in non-Arabic languages.

Key Takeaway
The copula serves to specify the relation to a particular subject, distinct from any implied relation in a derived predicate, thus it is not redundant.

Distinguishing Particular and Quantified Categorical Propositions
Author defines makhsusa and mahsura based on subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a proposition as makhsusa (particular) or mahsura (quantified) based on its subject type and the presence of a quantifier.

Core Idea
Where the subject is a real individual, the proposition is particular; where it is quantified, it is mahsura.

In this passage, the author introduces a fundamental division of categorical propositions based on the subject. You will learn to distinguish propositions that refer to a specific individual from those that make a claim about a universal quantified by words like 'every' or 'some'. This distinction is essential for analyzing arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the proposition
Look at the term the judgment is about. Is it a specific individual like 'Zayd' or a universal term like 'insan' (human)?
The subject is the term about which something is predicated.
2
Determine if the subject is a real particular
If the subject is a unique individual that cannot be shared, it is a juz'i haqiqi. Such propositions are called makhsusa or shakhsiyya.
Real particulars are concrete individuals, not conceptual classes.
3
Check for a quantifier (sawr)
If the subject is universal, look for words like kull (all) or ba'd (some). The presence of a quantifier makes it mahsura (quantified).
Quantifiers are also called sawr because they enclose the individuals like a wall.
4
Classify the proposition
Based on the above, label it as makhsusa or mahsura. If no quantifier and subject is universal, it is muhmala (unquantified), but that is beyond this passage.
This passage only covers makhsusa and mahsura; muhmala is discussed elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم شرع في تقسيم ثالث للحملية باعتبار الموضوع فقال: فموضوعها) أي موضوع الحملية إن كان شخصا معينا) أي: جزئيا حقيقيا (سميت) تلك القضية مخصوصة وشخصية؛ لكون موضوعها شخصا مخصوصا غير محتمل للاشتراك، كقولنا : زيد عالم (وإن كان الموضوع كليا فإن بين فيها مقدار) أي: كمية أفراد الموضوع من الكلية والبعضية، أي: حكم على جميع الأفراد أو على بعضها (سميت) القضية (محصورة)؛ لحصر موضوعها (ومسورة)؛ لاشتمالها على السور، (واللفظ الدال عليه أي على مقدار أفراد الموضوع (يسمى سورا)؛ لإحاطة الأفراد كإحاطة سور البلد.
Then he proceeded to a third division of the categorical [proposition] with respect to the subject, saying: So its subject (i.e., the subject of the categorical), if it is a specific individual (i.e., a real particular), it (that proposition) is called makhsusa and shakhsiyya, because its subject is a specific individual not susceptible to sharing, like our saying: Zayd is knowledgeable. And if the subject is a universal, then if the quantity (i.e., the amount of individuals of the subject, whether all or some) is specified (i.e., the judgment is on all individuals or on some of them), the proposition is called mahsura (because its subject is restricted) and musawwara (because it includes the sawr). And the word indicating it (i.e., indicating the quantity of the subject) is called sawr, because it encloses the individuals like the enclosure of the wall of a city.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage begins the third division of categorical propositions (hamliyya) based on the subject. The author states that if the subject is a specific individual (juz'i haqiqi), the proposition is called makhsusa or shakhsiyya, e.g., "Zayd is knowledgeable." If the subject is a universal (kulli), then the quantity (mikdar) of individuals must be specified, i.e., whether the judgment applies to all or some. Such a proposition is called mahsura (quantified) or musawwara (because it contains a sawr). The term sawr (quantifier) is defined as the word that indicates this quantity, analogous to a wall (sur) enclosing a city. The commentary explains that this division is crucial for understanding the logical form of propositions used in sciences like fiqh (e.g., "Every intoxicant is forbidden") and kalam.

The Four Quantified Propositions and Their Quantifiers
Author enumerates the four types of quantified categorical propositions with their quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of quantified categorical propositions and assign each its correct quantifier.

Core Idea
Four quantifiers unlock the logic of categorical propositions.

This lesson presents the four fundamental types of quantified propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Each type has specific quantifiers that determine how the judgment applies to the subject. Mastering these is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality and quantity
Determine if the proposition is affirmative or negative (quality) and if it applies to all or some individuals (quantity).
Quality refers to affirmation or negation; quantity refers to whether the judgment covers all or some.
2
Match the quantifier
For each combination of quality and quantity, there is a specific quantifier: universal affirmative uses 'كل', universal negative uses 'لا شيء' or 'لا واحد', particular affirmative uses 'بعض' or 'واحد', particular negative uses 'ليس كل', 'ليس بعض', or 'بعض ليس'.
The quantifiers are directly linked to the proposition type.
3
Check the nuance of 'ليس كل'
Note that 'ليس كل' is primarily a particular negative that denies the universal by direct implication (mutaabiqa) and the particular by entailment (iltizam). It can sometimes be used for universal negation.
This nuance prevents confusion between universal and particular negatives.
4
Practice with examples
Apply the classification to simple Arabic statements like those in the text: 'كل نار حارة', 'لا شيء من الناس بجماد', 'بعض الحيوان إنسان', 'ليس كل إنسان كاتباً'.
Regular practice builds fluency in identifying proposition types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وهي) أي: القضية المسورة المحصورة أربعة أقسام؛ لأنها (إما موجبة كلية) إن حكم فيها بالإيجاب على كل أفراد الموضوع (وسورها) أي: سور الموجبة الكلية (كل) الإفرادي لا المجموعي (كقولنا : كل نار حارة أي كل واحد من أفراد النار حارة، (وإما سالبة كلية) إن حكم فيها بالسلب عن كل الأفراد (وسورها) أي سور السالبة الكلية (لا شيء ولا واحد كقولنا : لا شيء ولا واحد من الناس بجماد، وإما موجبة جزئية) إن حكم فيها على بعض أفراد الموضوع بالإيجاب (وسورها) أي: سور الموجبة الجزئية (بعض وواحد كقولنا: بعض الحيوان أو واحد منه إنسان أي : بعض أفراده أو واحد من أفراده إنسان، (وإما سالبة جزئية) إن حكم فيها بالسلب عن بعض الأفراد (وسورها) أي: الجزئية (ليس كل وليس بعض وبعض ليس)، فالأول لسلب الحكم عن الكل بالمطابقة وعن البعض بالالتزام، والأخيران بالعكس، والأول منهما قد يذكر للسلب الكلي، والثاني بالعكس.
And it (i.e., the quantified proposition) is four divisions: because it is either universal affirmative if it judges affirmatively on every individual of the subject, and its quantifier is 'كل' (every) individually, not collectively, like our saying: Every fire is hot, i.e., every single individual of fire is hot. Or universal negative if it judges negatively on all individuals, and its quantifier is 'لا شيء' and 'لا واحد' like our saying: Nothing and no one of humans is inanimate. Or particular affirmative if it judges on some individuals of the subject affirmatively, and its quantifier is 'بعض' and 'واحد' like our saying: Some animal or one of them is human, i.e., some of its individuals or one of its individuals is human. Or particular negative if it judges negatively on some individuals, and its quantifiers (for the particular) are 'ليس كل' and 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس'. The first is for negating the judgment from the whole by direct implication and from the part by entailment, and the last two are the reverse. And the first of them may be used for universal negation, and the second for the reverse.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage details the four kinds of quantified propositions (mahsura). The author lists: (1) Universal affirmative (موجبة كلية) with quantifier 'كل' (every) as in 'Every fire is hot.' (2) Universal negative (سالبة كلية) with quantifiers 'لا شيء' or 'لا واحد' (nothing, no one) as in 'No human is inanimate.' (3) Particular affirmative (موجبة جزئية) with quantifiers 'بعض' or 'واحد' (some, one) as in 'Some animal is human.' (4) Particular negative (سالبة جزئية) with quantifiers 'ليس كل', 'ليس بعض', or 'بعض ليس' (not all, not some, some...not). The author explains the logical relationships: 'ليس كل' negates the universal (mutaabiqatan) and by implication the particular; the other two are the reverse. The first may also be used for universal negation, and the second for the reverse. This classification is foundational for syllogistic reasoning, as the quality and quantity determine the mood of a syllogism (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; this drink is intoxicant; therefore it is forbidden' uses a universal affirmative).

لا شيء
بعض
ليس كل

Key Takeaway
The four quantified propositions and their quantifiers form the backbone of categorical logic in the Islamic tradition.

Quantifier Placement and Deviant Propositions
Author notes that quantifiers are not fixed to the mentioned ones, should be placed on subject, and defines deviant propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a deviant proposition (munharifa) and explain why quantifiers should be placed on the subject.

Core Idea
A quantifier belongs on the subject; placing it on the predicate creates a deviant proposition.

In Islamic logic, quantifiers (sūr) are not universal across languages. They must be applied to the subject of a proposition, because the subject is what we quantify over. When a quantifier is applied to the predicate, the proposition becomes 'deviant' (munḥarifa). This lesson explains why that matters and how to recognize such propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify subject and predicate
In any categorical proposition, locate the subject (the thing being spoken about) and the predicate (what is said about it). For example, in 'Every believer is righteous,' 'believer' is subject, 'righteous' is predicate.
Use Islamic examples like 'All scholars are humble' or 'No prayer is invalid.'
2
Locate the quantifier
Find the quantifier word (e.g., 'every', 'no', 'some'). It should normally appear directly before the subject. If it appears before the predicate, that is a deviation.
Quantifiers can be implicit; the author says each language has its own.
3
Check quantifier placement
If the quantifier modifies the subject, the proposition is standard. If it modifies the predicate, it is deviant (munharifa). For example, 'Every scholar is humble' is standard; 'Humble is every scholar' is deviant.
Deviant propositions are still meaningful but logically non-standard.
4
Determine the four types of deviant propositions
The quantified predicate can be particular or universal, and the subject likewise. Multiplying yields four types. For instance, 'Some righteous are every believer' is one type.
The author does not elaborate all four; it's a lengthy discussion.
5
Understand why placement matters
The subject is what we often doubt the scope of (all or some). The predicate is a concept without multiplicity, so quantifying it is rare unless it has individuals. Placing quantifier on predicate deviates from the norm.
This preserves the clarity of logical analysis in syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس السور محصورا بما ذكر، بل في كل لغة سور مخصوص بها، ومن حق السور أن يرد على الموضوع؛ لأن الذي يحمل عليه الشيءُ قد يُشك في كون الحكم على كل أفراده أو على البعض، وقلما يتحقق (۱) ذلك في المحمول؛ لأن المراد منه المفهوم، ولا تعدد فيه حتى يُشك، اللهم إلا إذا جعل المحمول أفراده، فإذا أورد السور على المحمول فقد انحرف عن الواجب (۲)، فسميت القضية حينئذ منحرفة وأقسامها أربعة؛ لأن المحمول المسوَّر إما جزئي أو كلي، وكيفما كان فالموضوع أيضا كذلك، فتحصل أربعة أقسام بضرب الاثنين في الاثنين وبحث المنحرفات طويل الذيل لا يليق استقصاؤه بهذا المختصر .
And the quantifier is not confined to what was mentioned; rather, in every language there is a quantifier specific to it. And it is proper for the quantifier to be applied to the subject, because that which the thing is predicated of may be doubted as to whether the judgment is on all its individuals or on some, and that rarely occurs (1) in the predicate, because what is intended from it is the concept, and there is no multiplicity in it to cause doubt, except if the predicate is made to have individuals. So if the quantifier is applied to the predicate, then it has deviated from what is obligatory (2), so the proposition is then called 'deviant' (munharifa). And its divisions are four: because the quantified predicate is either particular or universal, and in any case the subject is likewise, so four divisions result by multiplying two by two. And the discussion of deviant propositions is lengthy; its thorough treatment is not suitable for this abridgment.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage extends the discussion of quantifiers. The author states that the quantifier (sawr) is not confined to those mentioned; each language has its own specific quantifiers. The proper place of a quantifier is on the subject, because the subject is what the predicate is asserted of, and it is often uncertain whether the judgment applies to all or some individuals. This uncertainty rarely applies to the predicate, because the predicate is a concept (mafhum) with no multiplicity. However, if the predicate is taken as having individuals, then placing the quantifier on the predicate results in a deviant (munharifa) proposition. The deviant proposition also has four divisions, obtained by multiplying the two possibilities for subject and predicate (each being either particular or universal). The author notes that a full discussion of deviant propositions is lengthy and not suitable for this abridgment. In Islamic logic, understanding quantifier placement is important for correct syllogistic forms; for example, 'Every scholar is humble' (subject quantifier) vs. deviant 'A scholar is every humble' (meaningless).

Key Takeaway

Natural and Indefinite Propositions
Author defines natural and indefinite propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between natural (ṭabī‘iyya) and indefinite (muhmala) propositions and identify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Natural propositions judge the nature itself; indefinite propositions leave quantity unspecified.

Logicians classify propositions not only by quality and quantity but also by how the subject is considered. Some propositions judge the very nature of the subject, like 'Animal is a genus,' while others leave the quantity of individuals open, like 'Humans are in loss.' Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting statements in fiqh and usul, where an indefinite statement is often treated as particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantity specification
Look at the proposition: is the quantity of subject individuals specified (universal/particular) or unspecified? If unspecified, proceed.
2
Determine the judgment's intention
If the proposition is not suitable to be intended as universal or particular—i.e., the judgment is on the nature (ṭabīʿa) itself—then it is natural. If it could be taken as universal or particular, it is indefinite.
Example: 'Animal is a genus' judges the nature of animal, not individual animals.
3
Classify the proposition
Apply the definition: natural if judgment on nature, indefinite if quantity is neglected but could be universal or particular.
Indefinite example: 'Humans are in loss' could be interpreted as all or some; hence indefinite.
4
Relate indefinite to particular
The text states that indefinite is equivalent in force to particular. Understand that whenever an indefinite is true, the corresponding particular is true, and vice versa.
This is a key logical equivalence used in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وإن لم يبين مقدار أفراد الموضوع (فإن لم يصلح القضية لأن تقصد بها كلية أو جزئية) بأن يكون الحكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع مطلقة أو مقيدة بالعموم (سميت) القضية التي حكم فيها على نفس طبيعة الموضوع (طبعية، وإلا) أي: وإن صلحت القضية لأن تصدق كلية أو جزئية (فمهملة) أي: سميت مهملة؛ لإهمال بيان كمية الأفراد فيها مع الاحتمال، كقولنا : الحيوان (جنس) مثال القضية الطبعية، فإنه حكم فيها على طبيعة الحيوان من حيث إنه عام وكقولنا الإنسان مقول، والحيوان مقوم(۳)، فإنه حكم فيها على طبيعة الإنسان والحيوان مطلقا، وكقولنا : (الإنسان في خسر) مثال القضية المهملة. (و) القضية المهملة في قوة الجزئية) يعنى أنهما متلازمتان؛ إذ متى صدق «الإنسان في خسر» صدق «بعض الإنسان في خسر وبالعكس) يعني متى صدق «بعض الإنسان في خسر » صدق «الإنسان في خسر» وذلك ظاهر. فإن قيل: بقي قسم آخر، وهو أن يكون الحكم على الأفراد والطبيعة معا، قلنا: إن بحثنا في القضايا المستعملة في العلوم والقضية التي يكون الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع
And if the quantity of the individuals of the subject is not specified: then if the proposition is not suitable to be intended as universal or particular (i.e., the judgment is on the nature itself, absolute or restricted to generality), it is called 'natural' (tabi'iyya) — the proposition in which the judgment is on the nature itself. And if not (i.e., the proposition can be true as universal or particular), then it is 'indefinite' (muhmala), called so because the quantity of individuals is neglected despite the possibility. Example of the natural proposition: 'Animal is a genus', for it judges on the nature of animal insofar as it is general; and also 'Human is [a predicate] and animal is [a constituent]', for it judges on the nature of human and animal absolutely. And example of the indefinite: 'Humans are in loss.' And the indefinite proposition is in the force of the particular, meaning they are mutually entailing: for whenever 'Humans are in loss' is true, 'Some humans are in loss' is true, and vice versa. That is evident. For if it is said: Another category remains, which is that the judgment be on the individuals and the nature together. We say: We are discussing the propositions used in the sciences, and the proposition in which the judgment is on the individuals of the subject...
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage covers two more types of categorical propositions based on whether the quantity of the subject is specified. If it is not specified, the proposition is either (a) natural (tabi'iyya): when the judgment is on the nature itself, not intended as universal or particular, e.g., 'Animal is a genus' (judging on the nature of animal) or 'Human is an inferior [predicable]' (judging on the nature); or (b) indefinite (muhmala): when the proposition could be taken as universal or particular, e.g., 'Humans are in loss.' The author then asserts that the indefinite is equivalent in force to the particular (fi quwwa al-juz'iyya), meaning they are mutually entailing: if 'Humans are in loss' is true, then 'Some humans are in loss' is true, and vice versa. A questioner objects that another category remains: a judgment on both individuals and nature together. The author begins to answer that the discussion concerns propositions used in the sciences, and the proposition in which the judgment is on the individuals of the subject... (text cuts off). This answer likely explains that such a case is covered by one of the existing types. In fiqh, an indefinite proposition like 'The scholars are fallible' is understood as particular in force, not universal.
